      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <b>
         <i>
          <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
           Reconquista
          </span>
         </i>
        </b>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [a]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (Spanish and Portuguese for the "reconquest") is the period in the history of the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       of about 780 years between the
       <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">
        Umayyad conquest of Hispania
       </a>
       in 711 and the
       <a href="/wiki/Granada_War#Last_stand_at_Granada" title="Granada War">
        fall of the Nasrid kingdom of Granada
       </a>
       to the expanding
       <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">
        Christian kingdoms
       </a>
       in 1492. The
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         Reconquista
        </span>
       </i>
       was completed just before the Spanish discovery of the
       <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">
        Americas
       </a>
       —the "
       <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">
        New World
       </a>
       "—which ushered in the era of the Spanish and Portuguese colonial empires. Since the mid-19th century, the idea of a 'reconquest' took hold in Spain associated with its rising
       <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">
        nationalism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">
        colonialism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-2">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Western historians have marked the beginning of the
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         Reconquista
        </span>
       </i>
       with the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Covadonga" title="Battle of Covadonga">
        Battle of Covadonga
       </a>
       (718 or 722), one of the first victories by Christian military forces since the 711 Islamic conquest of Iberia by the
       <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">
        Umayyad Caliphate
       </a>
       . In that small battle, a group led by the nobleman
       <a href="/wiki/Pelagius_of_Asturias" title="Pelagius of Asturias">
        Pelagius
       </a>
       defeated a caliphate's army in the mountains of northern Iberia and re-established the independent Christian
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Kingdom of Asturias
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_and_duration">
        Concept and duration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Concept and duration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Traditional
       <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">
        historiography
       </a>
       has stressed since the 19th century the existence of the
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-2">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a continuous phenomenon by which the Christian Iberian kingdoms opposed and conquered the Muslim kingdoms, understood as a common enemy who had militarily seized territory from native Iberian Christians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some contemporary authors consider it proved that the process of Christian state-building in Iberia was indeed often defined by the reclamation of lands that had been lost to the
       <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">
        Moors
       </a>
       in generations past. In this way, state-building might be characterised –at least in ideological, if not practical, terms– as a process by which Iberian states were being 'rebuilt'.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purkis_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Purkis-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The concept of a Christian reconquest of the peninsula first emerged, in tenuous form, at the end of the 9th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeMedieval_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CambridgeMedieval-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A landmark was set by the Christian
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chronica_Prophetica" title="Chronica Prophetica">
         Chronica Prophetica
        </a>
       </i>
       (883–884), a document stressing the Christian and Muslim cultural and religious divide in Iberia and the necessity to drive the Muslims out. About the same time, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cr%C3%B3nica_Albeldense" title="Crónica Albeldense">
        Crónica Albeldense
       </a>
       cites the notion of reconquering Iberia from its Muslim occupants in order to restore a united Christian Iberian polity such as it existed under the
       <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">
        Visigothic Kingdom
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purkis_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Purkis-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Almohad1200.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="716" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Almohad1200.png/220px-Almohad1200.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Almohad1200.png/330px-Almohad1200.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Almohad1200.png/440px-Almohad1200.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Almohad1200.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islami" title="Islami">
          Islamic
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohad_dynasty" title="Almohad dynasty">
          Almohad dynasty
         </a>
         and surrounding states, including the Christian Kingdoms of
         <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">
          Portugal
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">
          León
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">
          Castile
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">
          Navarre
         </a>
         , and the
         <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">
          Crown of Aragon
         </a>
         , c. 1200.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Both Christian and Muslim rulers fought amongst themselves. Alliances between Muslims and Christians were not uncommon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeMedieval_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CambridgeMedieval-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Blurring distinctions even further were the mercenaries from both sides who simply fought for whoever paid the most. The period is seen today to have had episodes of relative religious tolerance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
        Crusades
       </a>
       , which started late in the 11-12th century, bred the religious ideology of a Christian reconquest, confronted at that time with a similarly staunch Muslim
       <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">
        Jihad
       </a>
       ideology in
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">
        Al-Andalus
       </a>
       by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravids" title="Almoravids">
        Almoravids
       </a>
       , and to an even greater degree by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohads" title="Almohads">
        Almohads
       </a>
       . In fact, previous documents from the 10th and 11th centuries are mute on any idea of "reconquest".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Propaganda accounts of Muslim-Christian hostility came into being to support that idea, most notably the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chanson_de_Roland" title="Chanson de Roland">
         Chanson de Roland
        </a>
       </i>
       , a fictitious 11th-century French version of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Roncevaux_Pass_(778)" title="Battle of Roncevaux Pass (778)">
        Battle of Roncevaux Pass (778)
       </a>
       dealing with the Iberian
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">
         Saracens
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Moors
       </i>
       ), and taught as historical fact in the French educational system since 1880.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The modern idea of the earlier concept of
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       is inextricably linked to the foundational myths of
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_nationalism" title="Spanish nationalism">
        Spanish nationalism
       </a>
       in the 19th century, and consolidated by the mid-20th century during
       <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">
        Franco
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National-Catholicism" title="National-Catholicism">
        National-Catholic
       </a>
       dictatorship, based on a strong underlying Castilian ideological element.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-García_Fitz_2009,_pp._146-147_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-García_Fitz_2009,_pp._146-147-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The idea of a "liberation war" of
       <i>
        reconquest
       </i>
       against the Muslims, depicted as foreigners, suited well during the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">
        Spanish Civil War
       </a>
       the anti-Republican rebels agitating the banner of a Spanish fatherland threatened by regional nationalisms and communism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-García_Fitz_2009,_pp._146-147_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-García_Fitz_2009,_pp._146-147-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their rebellious pursuit was thus a Crusade for the restoration of Church's unity, where Franco stood for both
       <a href="/wiki/Pelagius_of_Asturias" title="Pelagius of Asturias">
        Pelagius of Asturias
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/El_Cid" title="El Cid">
        El Cid
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-García_Fitz_2009,_pp._146-147_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-García_Fitz_2009,_pp._146-147-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some recent historians dispute the whole concept of
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       as a concept created
       <i>
        a posteriori
       </i>
       in the service of later political goals. A few historians point out that Spain and Portugal did not previously exist as nations, and therefore the heirs of the Christian Visigothic Kingdom were not technically reconquering them, as the name suggests.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One of the first Spanish intellectuals to question the idea of a "reconquest" that lasted for eight centuries was
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ortega_y_Gasset" title="José Ortega y Gasset">
        José Ortega y Gasset
       </a>
       , writing in the first half of the 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the term is still widely in use.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
        Background
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_conquest_of_Christian_Iberia">
        Islamic conquest of Christian Iberia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Islamic conquest of Christian Iberia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">
        Umayyad conquest of Hispania
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guadalete" title="Battle of Guadalete">
        Battle of Guadalete
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 711, Muslim
       <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">
        Moors
       </a>
       , mainly North African
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">
        Berber
       </a>
       soldiers with some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
        Arabs
       </a>
       , crossed the
       <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">
        Strait of Gibraltar
       </a>
       and began their conquest of the
       <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">
        Visigothic Kingdom of Hispania
       </a>
       . After their conquest of the Visigothic kingdom's Iberian territories, the Muslims crossed the
       <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">
        Pyrenees
       </a>
       and took control of
       <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">
        Septimania
       </a>
       in 719, the last province of the Visigothic kingdom to be occupied. From their stronghold of
       <a href="/wiki/Narbonne" title="Narbonne">
        Narbonne
       </a>
       , they launched raids into the
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">
        Duchy of Aquitaine
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       At no point did the invading Islamic armies exceed 60,000 men.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These armies established an Islamic rule that would last 300 years in much of the Iberian Peninsula and 770 years in
       <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">
        Granada
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_rule">
        Islamic rule
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Islamic rule">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Berbers_and_Islam" title="Berbers and Islam">
        Berbers and Islam
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Berber_Revolt" title="Berber Revolt">
        Berber Revolt
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       After the establishment of a local
       <a href="/wiki/Emirate" title="Emirate">
        Emirate
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">
        Caliph
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Walid_I" title="Al-Walid I">
        Al-Walid I
       </a>
       , ruler of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">
        Umayyad caliphate
       </a>
       , removed many of the successful Muslim commanders.
       <a href="/wiki/Tariq_ibn_Ziyad" title="Tariq ibn Ziyad">
        Tariq ibn Ziyad
       </a>
       , the first governor of the newly conquered province of Al-Andalus, was recalled to
       <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">
        Damascus
       </a>
       and replaced with Musa bin Nusair, who had been his former superior. Musa's son, Abd al-Aziz ibn Musa, apparently married
       <a href="/wiki/Egilona" title="Egilona">
        Egilona
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Roderic" title="Roderic">
        Roderic
       </a>
       's widow, and established his regional government in
       <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">
        Seville
       </a>
       . He was suspected of being under the influence of his wife and was accused of wanting to convert to Christianity and of planning a secessionist rebellion. Apparently a concerned Al-Walid I ordered Abd al-Aziz's assassination. Caliph Al-Walid I died in 715 and was succeeded by his brother
       <a href="/wiki/Sulayman_ibn_Abd_al-Malik" title="Sulayman ibn Abd al-Malik">
        Sulayman ibn Abd al-Malik
       </a>
       . Sulayman seems to have punished the surviving Musa bin Nusair, who very soon died during a pilgrimage in 716. In the end, Abd al-Aziz ibn Musa's cousin,
       <a href="/wiki/Ayyub_ibn_Habib_al-Lakhmi" title="Ayyub ibn Habib al-Lakhmi">
        Ayyub ibn Habib al-Lakhmi
       </a>
       became the
       <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">
        emir
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        Al-Andalus
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The conquering generals were necessarily acting independently, due to the methods of communication available. Successful generals in the field and in a distant province would gain the personal loyalty of their officers and warriors and their ambitions were likely watched by certain circles of the distant government with a degree of concern and suspicion. Old rivalries and perhaps even full-fledged conspiracies between generals may have had influence over this development. In the end, the formerly successful generals were replaced by a younger generation considered more loyal to the government in Damascus.
      </p>
      <p>
       A serious weakness amongst the Muslim conquerors was the ethnic tension between Berbers and Arabs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Berbers were indigenous inhabitants of North Africa who had only recently converted to Islam; they provided most of the soldiery of the invading Islamic armies but sensed Arab discrimination against them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This latent internal conflict jeopardized Muslim unity.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the Islamic Moorish conquest of most of the Iberian Peninsula in 711–718 and the establishment of the emirate of Al-Andalus, an Umayyad expedition suffered a major defeat at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Toulouse_(721)" title="Battle of Toulouse (721)">
        Battle of Toulouse
       </a>
       and was halted for a while on its way north.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odo_of_Aquitaine" title="Odo of Aquitaine">
        Odo of Aquitaine
       </a>
       had married his daughter to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Naissa" title="Uthman ibn Naissa">
        Uthman ibn Naissa
       </a>
       , a rebel Berber and lord of
       <a href="/wiki/Cerdanya" title="Cerdanya">
        Cerdanya
       </a>
       (maybe of current Catalonia too), in an attempt to secure his southern borders in order to fend off
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">
        Charles Martel
       </a>
       's attacks on the north. However, a major
       <a href="/wiki/Punitive_expedition" title="Punitive expedition">
        punitive expedition
       </a>
       led by
       <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahman_Al_Ghafiqi" title="Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi">
        Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi
       </a>
       , the latest emir of Al-Andalus, defeated and killed Uthman, and the Muslim governor mustered an expedition north across the western Pyrenees, looted areas up to Bordeaux, and defeated Odo in the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_River_Garonne" title="Battle of the River Garonne">
        Battle of the River Garonne
       </a>
       in 732.
      </p>
      <p>
       A desperate Odo turned to his archrival Charles Martel for help, who led the Frankish and remaining Aquitanian armies against the Umayyad armies and defeated them at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">
        Battle of Tours
       </a>
       in 732, killing Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi. While Moorish rule began to recede, it would remain in parts of the Iberian peninsula for another 760 years.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_of_the_Reconquista">
        Beginning of the
        <i>
         Reconquista
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Beginning of the Reconquista">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Kingdom of Asturias
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="975" data-file-width="550" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg/200px-Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg/300px-Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg/400px-Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Escudo_de_Alcanadre-La_Rioja.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Coat of arms of
         <a href="/wiki/Alcanadre" title="Alcanadre">
          Alcanadre
         </a>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/La_Rioja_(Spain)" title="La Rioja (Spain)">
          La Rioja
         </a>
         , Spain. Depicting
         <a href="/wiki/Maure" title="Maure">
          heads of slain Moors
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first victory in resistance to Muslim rule occurred in
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Asturias
       </a>
       in 722. A drastic increase of taxes by the emir
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anbasa_ibn_Suhaym_Al-Kalbi" title="Anbasa ibn Suhaym Al-Kalbi">
        Anbasa ibn Suhaym Al-Kalbi
       </a>
       provoked several rebellions in Al-Andalus, which a series of succeeding weak emirs was unable to suppress. Around 722, a Muslim military expedition was sent into the north in late summer to suppress a rebellion led by
       <a href="/wiki/Pelagius_of_Asturias" title="Pelagius of Asturias">
        Pelagius of Asturias
       </a>
       (Pelayo in Spanish, Pelayu in Asturian). Traditional historiography has hailed Pelagius'
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Covadonga" title="Battle of Covadonga">
        victory at Covadonga
       </a>
       as the beginning of the
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       . No Muslim source mentions the battle at Covadonga, in contrast with the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Toulouse_(721)" title="Battle of Toulouse (721)">
        Battle of Toulouse
       </a>
       in 721, with a death toll of perhaps tens of thousands, which was mourned for centuries as a large scale tragedy by the Iberian Muslims. For Pelagius, however, the Christian victory secured his independent rule, while the precise date and circumstances of the battle are unclear. Among the possibilities is that Pelagius' rebellion was successful because the greater part of the Muslim forces were focusing in
       <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">
        Septimania
       </a>
       and Toulouse (721).
      </p>
      <p>
       Two northern realms, the Basque Navarre
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Asturias, despite their small size, demonstrated an ability to maintain their independence. Because the Umayyad rulers based in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Andalusia" title="Córdoba, Andalusia">
        Córdoba
       </a>
       were unable to extend their power over the Pyrenees, they decided to consolidate their power within the Iberian peninsula. Arab-Berber forces made periodic incursions deep into Asturias, but this area was a
       <i>
        cul-de-sac
       </i>
       on the fringes of the Islamic world fraught with inconveniences during campaigns and little interest.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It comes then as no surprise that, besides focusing on raiding the Arab-Berber strongholds of the Meseta, Alphonse I centred on expanding his domains at the expense of the neighbouring Galicians and Basques at either side of his realm just as much.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the first decades, Asturian control over part of the kingdom was weak, and for this reason it had to be continually strengthened through matrimonial alliances and war with other peoples from the north of the Iberian Peninsula. After Pelayo's death in 737, his son
       <a href="/wiki/Favila_of_Asturias" title="Favila of Asturias">
        Favila of Asturias
       </a>
       was elected king. Favila, according to the chronicles, was killed by a bear during a trial of courage. Pelayo's dynasty in Asturias survived and gradually expanded the kingdom's boundaries until all of northwest Iberia was included by roughly 775. However, credit is due not to him but to his successors, the
       <i>
        Banu Alfons
       </i>
       from the Arab chronicles. Further expansion of the northwestern kingdom towards the south occurred during the reign of Alfonso II (from 791–842). A king's expedition arrived in and pillaged Lisbon in 798, probably concerted with the Carolingians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Asturian kingdom became firmly established with the recognition of Alfonso II as king of Asturias by
       <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
        Charlemagne
       </a>
       and the Pope. During his reign, the bones of
       <a href="/wiki/James,_son_of_Zebedee" title="James, son of Zebedee">
        St. James the Great
       </a>
       were declared to have been found in Galicia, at
       <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_Compostela" title="Santiago de Compostela">
        Santiago de Compostela
       </a>
       . Pilgrims from all over Europe opened a channel of communication between the isolated Asturias and the Carolingian lands and beyond, centuries later.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Franks_and_Al-Andalus">
        Franks and Al-Andalus
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Franks and Al-Andalus">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_invasion_of_Gaul" title="Islamic invasion of Gaul">
        Islamic invasion of Gaul
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Marca_Hispanica" title="Marca Hispanica">
        Marca Hispanica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       After the Umayyad conquest of the Iberian heartland of the Visigothic kingdom, the Muslims crossed the Pyrenees and gradually took control of
       <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">
        Septimania
       </a>
       , starting in 719 with the conquest of
       <a href="/wiki/Narbonne" title="Narbonne">
        Narbonne
       </a>
       through 725 when
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcassone" title="Carcassone">
        Carcassone
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/N%C3%AEmes" title="Nîmes">
        Nîmes
       </a>
       were secured. From the stronghold of Narbonne, they tried to conquer
       <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">
        Aquitaine
       </a>
       but suffered a major defeat at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Toulouse_(721)" title="Battle of Toulouse (721)">
        Battle of Toulouse (721)
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_AR_20-33_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lewis_AR_20-33-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ten years after halting their advance north,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odo_of_Aquitaine" title="Odo of Aquitaine">
        Odo of Aquitaine
       </a>
       married his daughter to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Naissa" title="Uthman ibn Naissa">
        Uthman ibn Naissa
       </a>
       , a rebel Berber and lord of
       <a href="/wiki/Cerdanya" title="Cerdanya">
        Cerdanya
       </a>
       (perhaps of all of current Catalonia as well), in an attempt to secure his southern borders to fend off
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">
        Charles Martel
       </a>
       's attacks on the north. However, a major
       <a href="/wiki/Punitive_expedition" title="Punitive expedition">
        punitive expedition
       </a>
       led by
       <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahman_Al_Ghafiqi" title="Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi">
        Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi
       </a>
       , the latest emir of Al-Andalus, defeated and killed Uthman.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_AR_20-33_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lewis_AR_20-33-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pepin_the_Younger_and_Charlemagne">
        Pepin the Younger and Charlemagne
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pepin the Younger and Charlemagne">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       After expelling the Muslims
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Narbonne_(752-759)" title="Siege of Narbonne (752-759)">
        from Narbonne in 759
       </a>
       and driving their forces back over the Pyrenees, the Carolingian king
       <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short#Expansion_of_the_Frankish_realm" title="Pepin the Short">
        Pepin the Short conquered Aquitaine
       </a>
       in a ruthless eight-year war. Charlemagne followed his father by subduing Aquitaine by creating counties, taking the Church as his ally and appointing counts of Frankish or Burgundian stock, like his loyal
       <a href="/wiki/William_of_Gellone" title="William of Gellone">
        William of Gellone
       </a>
       , making
       <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">
        Toulouse
       </a>
       his base for expeditions against Al-Andalus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_AR_20-33_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lewis_AR_20-33-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Charlemagne decided to organize a regional subkingdom in order to keep the Aquitanians in check and to secure the southern border of the
       <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">
        Carolingian Empire
       </a>
       against Muslim incursions. In 781, his three-year-old son
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">
        Louis
       </a>
       was crowned king of
       <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">
        Aquitaine
       </a>
       , under the supervision of Charlemagne's trustee William of Gellone, and was nominally in charge of the incipient
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_March" title="Spanish March">
        Spanish March
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_AR_20-33_25-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lewis_AR_20-33-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Meanwhile, the takeover of the southern fringes of Al-Andalus by Abd ar-Rahman I in 756 was opposed by
       <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_ibn_%27Abd_al-Rahman_al-Fihri" title="Yusuf ibn 'Abd al-Rahman al-Fihri">
        Yusuf ibn Abd al-Rahman
       </a>
       , autonomous governor (
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W%C4%81li" title="Wāli">
         wāli
        </a>
       </i>
       ) or king (
       <i>
        malik
       </i>
       ) of al-Andalus. Abd ar-Rahman I expelled Yusuf from Cordova,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but it took still decades for him to expand to the north-western Andalusian districts. He was also opposed externally by the
       <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">
        Abbasids
       </a>
       of Baghdad who failed in their attempts to overthrow him. In 778, Abd al-Rahman closed in on the Ebro valley. Regional lords saw the Umayyad emir at the gates and decided to enlist the nearby Christian Franks. According to
       <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Athir" title="Ali ibn al-Athir">
        Ali ibn al-Athir
       </a>
       , a Kurdish historian of the 12th century, Charlemagne received the envoys of
       <a href="/wiki/Sulayman_al-Arabi" title="Sulayman al-Arabi">
        Sulayman al-Arabi
       </a>
       , Husayn, and
       <a href="/wiki/Abu_Taur_of_Huesca" title="Abu Taur of Huesca">
        Abu Taur
       </a>
       at the Diet of Paderborn in 777. These rulers of
       <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">
        Zaragoza
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Girona" title="Girona">
        Girona
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">
        Barcelona
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Huesca" title="Huesca">
        Huesca
       </a>
       were enemies of Abd ar-Rahman I, and in return for Frankish military aid against him offered their homage and allegiance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins,_Roger_1989_177–181_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Collins,_Roger_1989_177–181-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Charlemagne, seeing an opportunity, agreed upon an expedition and crossed the Pyrenees in 778. Near the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">
        Zaragoza
       </a>
       Charlemagne received the homage of
       <a href="/wiki/Sulayman_al-Arabi" title="Sulayman al-Arabi">
        Sulayman al-Arabi
       </a>
       . However the city, under the leadership of
       <a href="/wiki/Husayn_of_Zaragoza" title="Husayn of Zaragoza">
        Husayn
       </a>
       , closed its gates and refused to submit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins,_Roger_1989_177–181_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Collins,_Roger_1989_177–181-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unable to conquer the city by force, Charlemagne decided to retreat. On the way home the rearguard of the army was ambushed and destroyed by Basque forces at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Roncevaux_Pass" title="Battle of Roncevaux Pass">
        Battle of Roncevaux Pass
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_Roland" title="The Song of Roland">
        The Song of Roland
       </a>
       , a highly romanticized account of this battle, would later become one of the most famous
       <a href="/wiki/Chanson_de_geste" title="Chanson de geste">
        chansons de geste
       </a>
       of the Middle Ages. Around 788 Abd ar-Rahman I died and was succeeded by
       <a href="/wiki/Hisham_I_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Hisham I of Córdoba">
        Hisham I
       </a>
       . In 792 Hisham proclaimed a
       <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">
        jihad
       </a>
       , advancing in 793 against the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Kingdom of Asturias
       </a>
       and Carolingian
       <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">
        Septimania (Gothia)
       </a>
       . They defeated William of Gellone, Count of Toulouse, in battle, but
       <a href="/wiki/William_of_Gellone" title="William of Gellone">
        William
       </a>
       led an expedition the following year across the eastern Pyrenees.
       <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">
        Barcelona
       </a>
       , a major city, became a potential target for the Franks in 797, as its governor Zeid rebelled against the Umayyad emir of Córdoba. An army of the emir managed to recapture it in 799, but Louis, at the head of an army, crossed the Pyrenees and besieged the city for two years until it finally capitulated in 801.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_AR_37-49_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lewis_AR_37-49-28">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The main passes in the Pyrenees were
       <a href="/wiki/Roncesvalles" title="Roncesvalles">
        Roncesvalles
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Somport" title="Somport">
        Somport
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/La_Jonquera" title="La Jonquera">
        La Jonquera
       </a>
       . Charlemagne established across them the vassal regions of
       <a href="/wiki/Pamplona" title="Pamplona">
        Pamplona
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">
        Aragon
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">
        Catalonia
       </a>
       respectively. Catalonia was itself formed from a number of
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_counties" title="Catalan counties">
        small counties
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/County_of_Pallars" title="County of Pallars">
        Pallars
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Girona_(province)" title="Girona (province)">
        Girona
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Urgell" title="Urgell">
        Urgell
       </a>
       ; it was called the
       <i>
        Marca Hispanica
       </i>
       by the late 8th century. They protected the eastern Pyrenees passes and shores and were under the direct control of the Frankish kings. Pamplona's first king was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I%C3%B1igo_Arista" title="Iñigo Arista">
        Iñigo Arista
       </a>
       , who allied with his Muslim kinsmen the
       <a href="/wiki/Banu_Qasi" title="Banu Qasi">
        Banu Qasi
       </a>
       and rebelled against Frankish overlordship and overcame a
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Roncevaux_Pass_(824)" title="Battle of Roncevaux Pass (824)">
        Carolingian expedition in 824
       </a>
       that led to the setup of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pamplona" title="Kingdom of Pamplona">
        Kingdom of Pamplona
       </a>
       . Aragon, founded in 809 by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aznar_Gal%C3%ADndez" title="Aznar Galíndez">
        Aznar Galíndez
       </a>
       , grew around Jaca and the high valleys of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aragon_River" title="Aragon River">
        Aragon River
       </a>
       , protecting the old Roman road. By the end of the 10th century, Aragon was annexed by Navarre. Sobrarbe and Ribagorza were small counties and had little significance to the progress of the
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the late 9th century under
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilfred_the_Hairy,_Count_of_Barcelona" title="Wilfred the Hairy, Count of Barcelona">
        Count Wilfred
       </a>
       , Barcelona became the
       <i>
        de facto
       </i>
       capital of the region. It controlled the other counties' policies in a union, which led in 948 to the independence of Barcelona under
       <a href="/wiki/Borrell_II,_Count_of_Barcelona" title="Borrell II, Count of Barcelona">
        Count Borrel II
       </a>
       , who declared that the new dynasty in France (the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capet" title="Capet">
        Capets
       </a>
       ) were not the legitimate rulers of France nor, as a result, of his county. These states were small and, with the exception of Navarre, did not have the capacity for attacking the Muslims in the way that Asturias did, but their mountainous geography rendered them relatively safe from being conquered, and their borders remained stable for two centuries.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Military_culture_in_medieval_Iberia">
        Military culture in medieval Iberia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Military culture in medieval Iberia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           does not
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
            cite
           </a>
           any
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            sources
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit">
            improve this section
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            removed
           </a>
           .
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (December 2012)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In an atmosphere of constant conflict, warfare and daily life were strongly intertwined during this period. Small, lightly equipped armies reflected the need for society to be on constant alert. These forces were capable of moving long distances in short times, allowing a quick return home after sacking a target. Battles were mainly waged between clans, expelling intruder armies or sacking expeditions.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the context of the relative isolation of the Iberian Peninsula from the rest of Europe, and the contact with Moorish culture, geographical and cultural differences necessitated the use of military strategies, tactics, and equipment that were markedly different from those found in the rest of western Europe during this period.
      </p>
      <p>
       Medieval Iberian armies mainly comprised two types of forces: the cavalry (mostly nobles, but including commoner knights from the 10th century on) and the infantry, or
       <i>
        peones
       </i>
       (peasants). Infantry only went to war if needed, which was not common.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cavalry_and_infantry">
        Cavalry and infantry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cavalry and infantry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Iberian
       <a href="/wiki/Cavalry_tactics" title="Cavalry tactics">
        cavalry tactics
       </a>
       involved knights approaching the enemy, throwing
       <a href="/wiki/Javelin" title="Javelin">
        javelins
       </a>
       , then withdrawing to a safe distance before commencing another assault. Once the enemy formation was sufficiently weakened, the knights charged with thrusting
       <a href="/wiki/Spear" title="Spear">
        spears
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Lance" title="Lance">
        lances
       </a>
       did not arrive in Hispania until the 11th century). There were three types of knights (
       <i>
        caballeros
       </i>
       ): royal knights, noble knights (
       <i>
        caballeros
        <a href="/wiki/Hidalgo_(nobility)" title="Hidalgo (nobility)">
         hidalgos
        </a>
       </i>
       ), and commoner knights (
       <i>
        caballeros villanos
       </i>
       , or "mounted soldier from a
       <a href="/wiki/Villa#Post-Roman_era" title="Villa">
        villa
       </a>
       "). Royal knights were mainly nobles with a close relationship with the king, and thus claimed a direct Gothic inheritance.
      </p>
      <p>
       Royal knights were equipped in the same manner as their
       <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">
        Gothic
       </a>
       predecessors: mail hauberk, kite shield, a long
       <a href="/wiki/Sword" title="Sword">
        sword
       </a>
       (designed to fight from the horse), javelins, spears and a
       <a href="/wiki/Francisca" title="Francisca">
        Visigothic axe
       </a>
       . Noble knights came from the ranks of the
       <i>
        infanzones
       </i>
       or lower nobles, whereas the commoner knights were not noble but were wealthy enough to afford a horse. Uniquely in Europe, these horsemen comprised a militia cavalry force with no feudal links, being under the sole control of the king or the count of
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">
        Castile
       </a>
       because of
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fueros" title="Fueros">
         fueros
        </a>
       </i>
       (charters) with the crown. Both noble and common knights wore padded armour and carried javelins, spears and round-tasselled shields (influenced by Moorish shields), as well as a sword.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        peones
       </i>
       were
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peasants" title="Peasants">
        peasants
       </a>
       who went to battle in service of their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal" title="Feudal">
        feudal
       </a>
       lord. Poorly equipped, with bows and arrows, spears and short swords, they were mainly used as auxiliary troops. Their function in battle was to contain the enemy troops until the cavalry arrived and to block the enemy infantry from charging the knights. The
       <a href="/wiki/Longbow" title="Longbow">
        longbow
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Composite_bow" title="Composite bow">
        composite bow
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">
        crossbow
       </a>
       were the basic types of bows and were especially popular in the infantry.
      </p>
      <p>
       Armour was typically made of leather, with iron scales; full coats of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain_mail" title="Chain mail">
        chain mail
       </a>
       were extremely rare and horse
       <a href="/wiki/Barding" title="Barding">
        barding
       </a>
       completely unknown. Head protections consisted of a round helmet with nose protector (influenced by the designs used by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">
        Vikings
       </a>
       , who attacked during the 8th and 9th centuries) and a chain mail headpiece. Shields were often round or kidney-shaped, except for the kite-shaped designs used by the royal knights. Usually adorned with geometric designs, crosses or tassels, shields were made out of wood and had a leather cover.
      </p>
      <p>
       Steel swords were the most common weapon. The cavalry used long double-edged swords and the infantry short, single-edged ones. Guards were either semicircular or straight, but always highly ornamented with geometrical patterns. The spears and javelins were up to 1.5 metres long and had an iron tip. The double-axe — made of iron, 30 cm long, and possessing an extremely sharp edge — was designed to be equally useful as a thrown weapon or in close combat. Maces and hammers were not common, but some specimens have remained and are thought to have been used by members of the cavalry.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finally, mercenaries were an important factor, as many kings did not have enough soldiers available.
       <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">
        Norsemen
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flemings" title="Flemings">
        Flemish
       </a>
       spearmen, Frankish knights, Moorish mounted archers, and Berber light cavalry were the main types of mercenaries available and used in the conflict.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_changes">
        Technological changes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Technological changes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       This style of warfare remained dominant in the Iberian Peninsula until the late 11th century, when lance tactics entered from France, although the traditional horse javelin-shot techniques continued to be used. In the 12th and 13th centuries, soldiers typically carried a sword, a lance, a javelin, and either bow and arrows or crossbow and darts/bolts. Armor consisted of a coat of mail over a quilted jacket, extending at least to the knees, a helmet or iron cap, and bracers protecting the arms and thighs, either metal or leather.
      </p>
      <p>
       Shields were round or triangular, made of wood, covered with leather, and protected by an iron band; the shields of knights and nobles would bear the family's coat of arms. Knights rode in both the Muslim style,
       <i>
        a la jineta
       </i>
       (i.e. the equivalent of a modern jockey's seat), a short stirrup strap and bended knees allowed for better control and speed, or in the French style,
       <i>
        a la brida
       </i>
       , a long stirrup strap allowed for more security in the saddle (i.e. the equivalent of the modern cavalry seat, which is more secure) when acting as heavy cavalry. Horses were occasionally fitted with a coat of mail as well.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_into_the_Crusades_and_military_orders">
        Expansion into the Crusades and military orders
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Expansion into the Crusades and military orders">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">
        High Middle Ages
       </a>
       , the fight against the Moors in the Iberian Peninsula became linked to the fight of the whole of
       <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">
        Christendom
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       was originally a mere war of conquest. It only later underwent a significant shift in meaning toward a religiously justified war of liberation (see the Augustinian concept of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Just_War" title="Just War">
        Just War
       </a>
       ). The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">
        papacy
       </a>
       and the influential
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Cluny" title="Abbey of Cluny">
        Abbey of Cluny
       </a>
       in Burgundy not only justified the acts of war but actively encouraged Christian knights to seek armed confrontation with Moorish "infidels" instead of with each other.
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 11th century onwards
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indulgences" title="Indulgences">
        indulgences
       </a>
       were granted: In 1064
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_II" title="Pope Alexander II">
        Pope Alexander II
       </a>
       promised the participants of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_of_Barbastro" title="War of Barbastro">
        an expedition against Barbastro
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Tagr al-Andalus
       </i>
       , Aragon) a collective indulgence 30 years before
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">
        Pope Urban II
       </a>
       called the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">
        First Crusade
       </a>
       . Papal interest in Christian-Muslim relations in the peninsula are not without precedent — Popes Leo IV (847–855), John VIII (872–882) and John XIX (1024–33) are all known to have displayed substantial interest in the region. Not until 1095 and the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">
        Council of Clermont
       </a>
       did the
       <i>
        Reconquista
       </i>
       amalgamate the conflicting concepts of a peaceful pilgrimage and armed knight-errantry. But the papacy left no doubt about the heavenly reward for knights fighting for Christ (
       <i>
        militia Christi
       </i>
       ): in a letter, Urban II tried to persuade the
       <i>
        reconquistadores
       </i>
       fighting at
       <a href="/wiki/Tarragona" title="Tarragona">
        Tarragona
       </a>
       to stay in the Peninsula rather than joining the armed pilgrimage to conquer Jerusalem, saying that their contribution for Christianity was equally important. The pope promised them the same indulgences that he had promised to those who chose to join the First Crusade.
      </p>
      <p>
       Later
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_order_(society)" title="Military order (society)">
        military orders
       </a>
       like the
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Santiago" title="Order of Santiago">
        Order of Santiago
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Montesa" title="Order of Montesa">
        Montesa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Calatrava" title="Order of Calatrava">
        Order of Calatrava
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">
        Knights Templar
       </a>
       were founded or called to fight in Iberia. The Popes called the knights of Europe to the
       <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
        Crusades
       </a>
       in the peninsula. After the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alarcos" title="Battle of Alarcos">
        Disaster of Alarcos
       </a>
       , French, Navarrese, Castilian, Portuguese and Aragonese armies united against the Muslim forces in the massive
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Navas_de_Tolosa" title="Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa">
         battle of Las Navas de Tolosa
        </a>
       </i>
       (1212). The large territories awarded to military orders and nobles were the origin of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latifundia" title="Latifundia">
        latifundia
       </a>
       in today's
       <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">
        Andalusia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Extremadura" title="Extremadura">
        Extremadura
       </a>
       in Spain, and
       <a href="/wiki/Alentejo" title="Alentejo">
        Alentejo
       </a>
       in Portugal.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Christian_realms">
        Northern Christian realms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Northern Christian realms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The northern principalities and kingdoms survived in their mountainous strongholds (see above). However, they started a definite territorial expansion south at the turn of the 10th century (Leon, Najera). The fall of the Caliphate of Cordova (1031) heralded a period of military expansion for the northern kingdoms, now divided into several mighty regional powers after the division of the Kingdom of Navarre (1035). A myriad of autonomous Christian kingdoms emerged thereafter.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Kingdom_of_Asturias_.28718.E2.80.93924.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Asturias_(718–924)">
        Kingdom of Asturias (718–924)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Asturias (718–924)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Kingdom of Asturias
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia" title="Kingdom of Galicia">
        Kingdom of Galicia
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Cantabria" title="Duchy of Cantabria">
        Duchy of Cantabria
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Kingdom of Asturias was located in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cantabrian_Mountains" title="Cantabrian Mountains">
        Cantabrian Mountains
       </a>
       , a wet and mountainous region in the north of the Iberian Peninsula. It was the first Christian power to emerge. The kingdom was established by a nobleman,
       <a href="/wiki/Pelagius_of_Asturias" title="Pelagius of Asturias">
        Pelagius
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Pelayo
       </i>
       ), who had possibly returned after the Battle of Guadalete in 711 and was elected leader of the Asturians, laying the foundations for the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Kingdom of Asturias
       </a>
       and starting the
       <a href="/wiki/Astur-Leonese_dynasty" title="Astur-Leonese dynasty">
        Astur-Leonese dynasty
       </a>
       that spanned from 718 to 1037 and led the initial efforts in the Iberian peninsula to take back the territories then ruled by the Moors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although the new dynasty first ruled in the mountains of Asturias, with the capital of the kingdom established initially in
       <a href="/wiki/Cangas_de_On%C3%ADs" title="Cangas de Onís">
        Cangas de Onís
       </a>
       , and was in its dawn mostly concerned with securing the territory and settling the monarchy, the latest kings (particularly
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_III_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso III of Asturias">
        Alfonso III of Asturias
       </a>
       ) emphasized the nature of the new kingdom as heir of that in
       <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">
        Toledo
       </a>
       and the restoration of the Visigothic nation in order to vindicate the expansion to the south.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, such claims have been overall dismissed by modern historiography, emphasizing the distinct, autochthonous nature of the Cantabro-Asturian and Vasconic domains with no continuation to the Gothic Kingdom of Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Pelagius' kingdom initially was little more than a gathering point for the existing guerrilla forces. During the first decades, the Asturian dominion over the different areas of the kingdom was still lax, and for this reason it had to be continually strengthened through matrimonial alliances with other powerful families from the north of the Iberian Peninsula. Thus, Ermesinda, Pelagius' daughter, was married to
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_I_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso I of Asturias">
        Alfonso
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Cantabria" title="Duchy of Cantabria">
        Dux Peter of Cantabria
       </a>
       's son. Alfonso's son
       <a href="/wiki/Fruela_I_of_Asturias" title="Fruela I of Asturias">
        Fruela
       </a>
       married Munia, a Basque from
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lava" title="Álava">
        Álava
       </a>
       , after crushing a Basque uprising (probably resistance). Their son is reported to be
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_II_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso II of Asturias">
        Alfonso II
       </a>
       , while Alfonso I's daughter Adosinda married Silo, a local chief from the area of Flavionavia, Pravia.
      </p>
      <p>
       Alfonso's military strategy was typical of Iberian warfare at the time. Lacking the means needed for wholesale conquest of large territories, his tactics consisted of raids in the border regions of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vardulia" title="Vardulia">
        Vardulia
       </a>
       . With the plunder he gained further military forces could be paid, enabling him to raid the Muslim cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">
        Lisbon
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zamora_(Spain)" title="Zamora (Spain)">
        Zamora
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Coimbra" title="Coimbra">
        Coimbra
       </a>
       . Alfonso I also expanded his realm westwards conquering
       <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">
        Galicia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="420" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg/220px-Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg/330px-Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Painting_of_Santiago_Matamoros.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/James,_son_of_Zebedee#Spain" title="James, son of Zebedee">
          St James the Great
         </a>
         , depicted as
         <a href="/wiki/Saint_James_Matamoros" title="Saint James Matamoros">
          Santiago Matamoros
         </a>
         (Santiago the Moor-slayer)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_II_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso II of Asturias">
        King Alfonso II
       </a>
       (791–842), the kingdom was firmly established, and a series of Muslim raids caused the transfer of the Asturian capital to
       <a href="/wiki/Oviedo" title="Oviedo">
        Oviedo
       </a>
       . The king is believed to have initiated diplomatic contacts with the kings of
       <a href="/wiki/Pamplona" title="Pamplona">
        Pamplona
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">
        Carolingians
       </a>
       , thereby gaining official recognition for his kingdom and his crown from the
       <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">
        Pope
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
        Charlemagne
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">
        bones
       </a>
       of St.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_the_Great" title="James the Great">
        James the Great
       </a>
       were proclaimed to have been found in Iria Flavia (present day
       <a href="/wiki/Padr%C3%B3n" title="Padrón">
        Padrón
       </a>
       ) in 813 or probably two or three decades later. The cult of the saint was transferred later to
       <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_Compostela" title="Santiago de Compostela">
        Compostela
       </a>
       (from Latin
       <i>
        campus stellae
       </i>
       , literally "the star field"), possibly in the early 10th century when the focus of Asturian power moved from the mountains over to León, to become the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">
        Kingdom of León
       </a>
       or Galicia-León. Santiago's were among many saint relics proclaimed to have been found across north-western Iberia. Pilgrims started to flow in from other Iberian Christian realms, sowing the seeds of the later
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Way_of_Saint_James" title="Way of Saint James">
        Way of Saint James
       </a>
       (11–12th century) that sparked the enthusiasm and religious zeal of continental
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Europe" title="Christian Europe">
        Christian Europe
       </a>
       for centuries.
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite numerous battles, neither the Umayyads nor the Asturians had sufficient forces to secure control over these northern territories. Under the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Ramiro_I_of_Asturias" title="Ramiro I of Asturias">
        Ramiro
       </a>
       , famed for the highly legendary
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Clavijo" title="Battle of Clavijo">
        Battle of Clavijo
       </a>
       , the border began to slowly move southward and Asturian holdings in
       <a href="/wiki/Castile_(historical_region)" title="Castile (historical region)">
        Castile
       </a>
       , Galicia, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n_(province)" title="León (province)">
        León
       </a>
       were fortified, and an intensive program of re-population of the countryside began in those territories. In 924 the Kingdom of Asturias became the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">
        Kingdom of León
       </a>
       , when León became the seat of the royal court (it didn't bear any official name).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Kingdom_of_Le.C3.B3n_.28910.E2.80.931230.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_León_(910–1230)">
        Kingdom of León (910–1230)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Kingdom of León (910–1230)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">
        Kingdom of León
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia" title="Kingdom of Galicia">
        Kingdom of Galicia
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/County_of_Portugal" title="County of Portugal">
        County of Portugal
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_III_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso III of Asturias">
        Alfonso III of Asturias
       </a>
       repopulated the strategically important city
       <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Spain" title="León, Spain">
        León
       </a>
       and established it as his capital. King Alfonso began a series of campaigns to establish control over all the lands north of the
       <a href="/wiki/Douro" title="Douro">
        Douro
       </a>
       river. He reorganized his territories into the major duchies (
       <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">
        Galicia
       </a>
       and Portugal) and major counties (
       <a href="/wiki/Salda%C3%B1a,_Palencia" title="Saldaña, Palencia">
        Saldaña
       </a>
       and Castile), and fortified the borders with many castles. At his death in 910 the shift in regional power was completed as the kingdom became the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">
        Kingdom of León
       </a>
       . From this power base, his heir
       <a href="/wiki/Ordo%C3%B1o_II_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Ordoño II of León">
        Ordoño II
       </a>
       was able to organize attacks against
       <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Spain" title="Toledo, Spain">
        Toledo
       </a>
       and even
       <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">
        Seville
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">
        Caliphate of Córdoba
       </a>
       was gaining power, and began to attack León. King Ordoño allied with Navarre against Abd-al-Rahman, but they were
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Valdejunquera" title="Battle of Valdejunquera">
        defeated in Valdejunquera
       </a>
       in 920. For the next 80 years, the Kingdom of León suffered civil wars, Moorish attack, internal intrigues and assassinations, and the partial independence of Galicia and Castile, thus delaying the reconquest and weakening the Christian forces. It was not until the following century that the Christians started to see their conquests as part of a long-term effort to restore the unity of the Visigothic kingdom.
      </p>
      <p>
       The only point during this period when the situation became hopeful for León was the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Ramiro_II_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Ramiro II of León">
        Ramiro II
       </a>
       . King Ramiro, in alliance with
       <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A1n_Gonz%C3%A1lez_of_Castile" title="Fernán González of Castile">
        Fernán González of Castile
       </a>
       and his retinue of
       <i>
        caballeros villanos
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Simancas" title="Battle of Simancas">
        defeated the Caliph in Simancas
       </a>
       in 939. After this battle, when the Caliph barely escaped with his guard and the rest of the army was destroyed, King Ramiro obtained 12 years of peace, but he had to give González the independence of Castile as payment for his help in the battle. After this defeat, Moorish attacks abated until
       <a href="/wiki/Almanzor" title="Almanzor">
        Almanzor
       </a>
       began his campaigns.
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_V_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Alfonso V of León">
        Alfonso V
       </a>
       finally regained control over his domains in 1002. Navarre, though attacked by Almanzor, remained intact.
      </p>
      <p>
       The conquest of León did not include Galicia which was left to temporary independence after the withdrawal of the Leonese king. Galicia was conquered soon after (by Ferdinand, son of Sancho the Great, around 1038). However, this brief period of independence meant that Galicia was remained kingdom and fief of Leon which is the reason it is part of Spain and not Portugal. Subsequent kings titled themselves kings of Galicia and León, instead of merely king of León as the two were united personally and not in union.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Kingdom_of_Castile_.281037.E2.80.931230.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Castile_(1037–1230)">
        Kingdom of Castile (1037–1230)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconquista&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Castile (1037–1230)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">
        Kingdom of Castile
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Ferdinand I of León">
        Ferdinand I of León
       </a>
       was the leading king of the mid-11th century. He conquered
       <a href="/wiki/Coimbra" title="Coimbra">
        Coimbra
       </a>
       and attacked the
       <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">
        taifa
       </a>
       kingdoms, often demanding the tributes known as
       <a href="/wiki/Parias" title="Parias">
        parias
       </a>
       . Ferdinand's strategy was to continue to demand parias until the taifa was greatly weakened both militarily and financially. He also repopulated the Borders with numerous
       <i>
        fueros
       </i>
       . Following the Navarrese tradition, on his death in 1064 he divided his kingdom between his sons. His son
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sancho_II_of_Castile" title="Sancho II of Castile">
        Sancho II of Castile
       </a>
       wanted to reunite the kingdom of his father and attacked his brothers, with a young noble at his side: Rodrigo Díaz, later known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Cid_Campeador" title="El Cid Campeador">
        El Cid Campeador
       </a>
       . Sancho was killed in the siege of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zamora_(Spain)" title="Zamora (Spain)">
        Zamora
       </a>
       by the traitor Bellido Dolfos (also known as Vellido Adolfo) in 1072. His brother
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfonso_VI_of_Castile" title="Alfonso VI of Castile">
        Alfonso VI
       </a>
       took over León, Castile and Galicia.
      </p>
      <p>
       Alfonso VI the Brave gave more power to the
       <i>
        fueros
       </i>
       and repopulated
       <a href="/wiki/Segovia" title="Segovia">
        Segovia
       </a>
       ,
